.navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_aluminium" title="Template:Infobox aluminium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_aluminium" title="Template talk:Infobox aluminium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Aluminium</b> (<b>aluminum</b> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AmE" title="AmE">American</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CanE" title="CanE">Canadian English</a>) is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbol_(chemistry)" title="Symbol (chemistry)">symbol</a> <b>Al</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 13. Aluminium has a density lower than those of other common <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a>, at approximately one third that of <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>. It has a great affinity towards <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">forms a protective layer</a> of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">oxide</a> on the surface when exposed to air. Aluminium visually resembles silver, both in its color and in its great ability to reflect light. It is soft, <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">non-magnetic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductile</a>. It has one stable isotope, <sup>27</sup>Al; this isotope is very common, making aluminium the twelfth most common element in the Universe. The radioactivity of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminum-26" title="Aluminum-26"><sup>26</sup>Al</a> is used in <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">radiodating</a>.
</p><p>Chemically, aluminium is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-transition_metal" title="Post-transition metal">post-transition metal</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Boron_group" title="Boron group">boron group</a>; as is common for the group, aluminium forms compounds primarily in the +3 <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a>. The aluminium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cation" title="Cation">cation</a> Al<sup>3+</sup> is small and highly charged; as such, it is polarizing, and <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">bonds</a> aluminium forms tend towards <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalency</a>. The strong affinity towards oxygen leads to aluminium's common association with oxygen in nature in the form of oxides; for this reason, aluminium is found on Earth primarily in rocks in the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" title="Earth's crust">crust</a>, where it is the third most abundant element after <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>, rather than in the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a>, and virtually never as the free metal.
</p><p>The discovery of aluminium was announced in 1825 by Danish physicist <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted" title="Hans Christian Ørsted">Hans Christian Ørsted</a>. The first industrial production of aluminium was initiated by French chemist <a href="/wiki/Henri_%C3%89tienne_Sainte-Claire_Deville" title="Henri Étienne Sainte-Claire Deville">Henri Étienne Sainte-Claire Deville</a> in 1856. Aluminium became much more available to the public with the <a href="/wiki/Hall%E2%80%93H%C3%A9roult_process" title="Hall–Héroult process">Hall–Héroult process</a> developed independently by French engineer <a href="/wiki/Paul_H%C3%A9roult" title="Paul Héroult">Paul Héroult</a> and American engineer <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martin_Hall" title="Charles Martin Hall">Charles Martin Hall</a> in 1886, and the mass production of aluminium led to its extensive use in industry and everyday life. In World Wars <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">I</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">II</a>, aluminium was a crucial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strategic_resource" title="Strategic resource">strategic resource</a> for <a href="/wiki/Aviation" title="Aviation">aviation</a>. In 1954, aluminium became the most produced <a href="/wiki/Non-ferrous_metal" title="Non-ferrous metal">non-ferrous metal</a>, surpassing <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>. In the 21st century, most aluminium was consumed in transportation, engineering, construction, and packaging in the United States, Western Europe, and Japan.
</p><p>Despite its prevalence in the environment, no living organism is known to use aluminium <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metabolically" title="Metabolically">metabolically</a>, but aluminium is well tolerated by plants and animals. Because of the abundance of these salts, the potential for a biological role for them is of interest, and studies continue.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Production_and_refinement"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Production and refinement</span></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_aluminium" title="Isotopes of aluminium">Isotopes of aluminium</a></div>
<p>Of aluminium isotopes, only <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">27</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Al<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> is stable. This situation is common for elements with an odd atomic number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[b]</a></sup> It is the only <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial</a> aluminium isotope, i.e. the only one that has existed on Earth in its current form since the formation of the planet. Nearly all aluminium on Earth is present as this isotope, which makes it a <a href="/wiki/Mononuclidic_element" title="Mononuclidic element">mononuclidic element</a> and means that its <a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">standard atomic weight</a> is virtually the same as that of the isotope. This makes aluminium very useful in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance">nuclear magnetic resonance</a> (NMR), as its single stable isotope has a high NMR sensitivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997242–252_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997242–252-9">[7]</a></sup> The standard atomic weight of aluminium is low in comparison with many other metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[c]</a></sup>
</p><p>All other isotopes of aluminium are <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive</a>. The most stable of these is <a href="/wiki/Aluminium-26" title="Aluminium-26"><sup>26</sup>Al</a>: while it was present along with stable <sup>27</sup>Al in the interstellar medium from which the Solar System formed, having been produced by <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a> as well, its <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> is only 717,000 years and therefore a detectable amount has not survived since the formation of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[9]</a></sup> However, minute traces of <sup>26</sup>Al are produced from <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a> by <a href="/wiki/Spallation" title="Spallation">spallation</a> caused by <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic ray</a> protons. The ratio of <sup>26</sup>Al to <a href="/wiki/Beryllium-10" title="Beryllium-10"><sup>10</sup>Be</a> has been used for <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">radiodating</a> of geological processes over 10<sup>5</sup> to 10<sup>6</sup> year time scales, in particular transport, deposition, <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a> storage, burial times, and erosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[10]</a></sup> Most meteorite scientists believe that the energy released by the decay of <sup>26</sup>Al was responsible for the melting and <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">differentiation</a> of some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroids" title="Asteroids">asteroids</a> after their formation 4.55 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The remaining isotopes of aluminium, with <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass numbers</a> ranging from 22 to 43, all have half-lives well under an hour. Three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metastable" title="Metastable">metastable</a> states are known, all with half-lives under a minute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEA_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-IAEA-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electron_shell">Electron shell</span></h3>
<p>An aluminium atom has 13 electrons, arranged in an <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">electron configuration</a> of <span class="nowrap">[<a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">Ne</a>] 3s<sup>2</sup> 3p<sup>1</sup></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean19994.2_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean19994.2-15">[12]</a></sup> with three electrons beyond a stable noble gas configuration. Accordingly, the combined first three <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">ionization energies</a> of aluminium are far lower than the fourth ionization energy alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean19994.6_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean19994.6-16">[13]</a></sup> Such an electron configuration is shared with the other well-characterized members of its group, <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">indium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thallium" title="Thallium">thallium</a>; it is also expected for <a href="/wiki/Nihonium" title="Nihonium">nihonium</a>. Aluminium can relatively easily surrender its three outermost electrons in many chemical reactions (see <a href="#Chemistry">below</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a> of aluminium is 1.61 (Pauling scale).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean19994.29_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean19994.29-17">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aluminium_Atomic_lattice.png"><img alt="M. Tunes &amp; S. Pogatscher, Montanuniversität Leoben 2019 No copyrights =)" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Aluminium_Atomic_lattice.png/260px-Aluminium_Atomic_lattice.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Aluminium_Atomic_lattice.png/390px-Aluminium_Atomic_lattice.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d9/Aluminium_Atomic_lattice.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aluminium_Atomic_lattice.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>High-resolution <a href="/wiki/Scanning_transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Scanning transmission electron microscopy">STEM</a>-<a href="/wiki/Annular_dark-field_imaging" title="Annular dark-field imaging">HAADF</a> micrograph of Al atoms viewed along the [001] zone axis.</div></div></div>
<p>A free aluminium atom has a <a href="/wiki/Atomic_radius" title="Atomic radius">radius</a> of 143 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picometer" title="Picometer">pm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean19994.30_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean19994.30-18">[15]</a></sup> With the three outermost electrons removed, the <a href="/wiki/Ionic_radius" title="Ionic radius">radius</a> shrinks to 39 pm for a 4-coordinated atom or 53.5 pm for a 6-coordinated atom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean19994.30_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean19994.30-18">[15]</a></sup> At <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a>, aluminium atoms (when not affected by atoms of other elements) form a <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">face-centered cubic crystal system</a> bound by <a href="/wiki/Metallic_bonding" title="Metallic bonding">metallic bonding</a> provided by atoms' outermost electrons; hence aluminium (at these conditions) is a metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enghag2008_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Enghag2008-19">[16]</a></sup> This crystal system is shared by many other metals, such as <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>; the size of a unit cell of aluminium is comparable to that of those other metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enghag2008_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Enghag2008-19">[16]</a></sup> The system, however, is not shared by the other members of its group; boron has ionization energies too high to allow metallization, thallium has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexagonal_close-packed" title="Hexagonal close-packed">hexagonal close-packed</a> structure, and gallium and indium have unusual structures that are not close-packed like those of aluminium and thallium. The few electrons that are available for <a href="/wiki/Metallic_bonding" title="Metallic bonding">metallic bonding</a> in aluminium metal are a probable cause for it being soft with a low melting point and low <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistivity" title="Electrical resistivity">electrical resistivity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood222_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood222-20">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bulk">Bulk</span></h3>
<p>Aluminium metal has an appearance ranging from silvery white to dull gray, depending on the <a href="/wiki/Surface_roughness" title="Surface roughness">surface roughness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[d]</a></sup> Aluminium mirrors are the most reflective of all metal mirrors for the near <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> and far <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> light, and one of the most reflective in the visible spectrum, nearly on par with silver, and the two therefore look similar. Aluminium is also good at reflecting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a>, although prolonged exposure to sunlight in air adds wear to the surface of the metal; this may be prevented if aluminium is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anodization" title="Anodization">anodized</a>, which adds a protective layer of oxide on the surface.
</p><p>The density of aluminium is 2.70 g/cm<sup>3</sup>, about 1/3 that of steel, much lower than other commonly encountered metals, making aluminium parts easily identifiable through their lightness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELide20044-3_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELide20044-3-24">[20]</a></sup> Aluminium's low density compared to most other metals arises from the fact that its nuclei are much lighter, while difference in the unit cell size does not compensate for this difference. The only lighter metals are the metals of <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">groups 1</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">2</a>, which apart from <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> are too reactive for structural use (and beryllium is very toxic).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[21]</a></sup> Aluminium is not as strong or stiff as steel, but the low density makes up for this in the <a href="/wiki/Aerospace" title="Aerospace">aerospace</a> industry and for many other applications where light weight and relatively high strength are crucial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis19991–3_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis19991–3-26">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pure aluminium is quite soft and lacking in strength. In most applications various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium_alloys" title="Aluminium alloys">aluminium alloys</a> are used instead because of their higher strength and hardness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis19992_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis19992-27">[23]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Yield_(engineering)" title="Yield (engineering)">yield strength</a> of pure aluminium is 7–11 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">MPa</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_alloy" title="Aluminium alloy">aluminium alloys</a> have yield strengths ranging from 200 MPa to 600 MPa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polmear1995_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polmear1995-28">[24]</a></sup> Aluminium is <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductile</a>, with a percent elongation of 50-70%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cardarelli_2008_p158-163_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cardarelli_2008_p158-163-29">[25]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malleable" title="Malleable">malleable</a> allowing it to be easily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drawing_(metalworking)" title="Drawing (metalworking)">drawn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Extrusion" title="Extrusion">extruded</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis19994_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis19994-30">[26]</a></sup> It is also easily <a href="/wiki/Machining" title="Machining">machined</a> and <a href="/wiki/Casting_(metalworking)" title="Casting (metalworking)">cast</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis19994_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis19994-30">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium is an excellent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_conduction" title="Heat conduction">thermal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">electrical conductor</a>, having around 60% the conductivity of <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, both thermal and electrical, while having only 30% of copper's density.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis19992–3_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis19992–3-31">[27]</a></sup> Aluminium is capable of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superconductor" title="Superconductor">superconductivity</a>, with a superconducting critical temperature of 1.2 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a> and a critical magnetic field of about 100 <a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">gauss</a> (10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millitesla" title="Millitesla">milliteslas</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[28]</a></sup> It is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paramagnetic" title="Paramagnetic">paramagnetic</a> and thus essentially unaffected by static magnetic fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchmitz20066_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmitz20066-33">[29]</a></sup> The high electrical conductivity, however, means that it is strongly affected by alternating magnetic fields through the induction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eddy_currents" title="Eddy currents">eddy currents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchmitz2006161_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmitz2006161-34">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compounds_of_aluminium" title="Compounds of aluminium">Compounds of aluminium</a></div>
<p>Aluminium combines characteristics of pre- and post-transition metals. Since it has few available electrons for metallic bonding, like its heavier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Group_13_element" title="Group 13 element">group 13</a> congeners, it has the characteristic physical properties of a post-transition metal, with longer-than-expected interatomic distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood222_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood222-20">[17]</a></sup> Furthermore, as Al<sup>3+</sup> is a small and highly charged cation, it is strongly polarizing and <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">bonding</a> in aluminium compounds tends towards <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalency</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227-35">[31]</a></sup> this behavior is similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a> (Be<sup>2+</sup>), and the two display an example of a <a href="/wiki/Diagonal_relationship" title="Diagonal relationship">diagonal relationship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997112–113_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997112–113-36">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The underlying core under aluminium's valence shell is that of the preceding <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a>, whereas those of its heavier congeners <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">indium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thallium" title="Thallium">thallium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nihonium" title="Nihonium">nihonium</a> also include a filled d-subshell and in some cases a filled f-subshell. Hence, the inner electrons of aluminium shield the valence electrons almost completely, unlike those of aluminium's heavier congeners. As such, aluminium is the most electropositive metal in its group, and its hydroxide is in fact more basic than that of gallium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227-35">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[e]</a></sup> Aluminium also bears minor similarities to the metalloid boron in the same group: AlX<sub>3</sub> compounds are valence <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isoelectronic" title="Isoelectronic">isoelectronic</a> to BX<sub>3</sub> compounds (they have the same valence electronic structure), and both behave as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acids</a> and readily form <a href="/wiki/Adduct" title="Adduct">adducts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing1995241_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing1995241-38">[33]</a></sup> Additionally, one of the main motifs of boron chemistry is <a href="/wiki/Regular_icosahedron" title="Regular icosahedron">regular icosahedral</a> structures, and aluminium forms an important part of many icosahedral <a href="/wiki/Quasicrystal" title="Quasicrystal">quasicrystal</a> alloys, including the Al–Zn–Mg class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing1995235–236_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing1995235–236-39">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium has a high <a href="/wiki/Chemical_affinity" title="Chemical affinity">chemical affinity</a> to oxygen, which renders it suitable for use as a <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Thermite" title="Thermite">thermite</a> reaction. A fine powder of aluminium metal reacts explosively on contact with <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a>; under normal conditions, however, aluminium forms a thin oxide layer (~5 nm at room temperature)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[35]</a></sup> that protects the metal from further corrosion by oxygen, water, or dilute acid, a process termed <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227-35">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CorrAl_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-CorrAl-41">[36]</a></sup> Because of its general resistance to corrosion, aluminium is one of the few metals that retains silvery reflectance in finely powdered form, making it an important component of <a href="/wiki/Silver_(color)" title="Silver (color)">silver-colored</a> paints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[37]</a></sup> Aluminium is not attacked by oxidizing acids because of its passivation. This allows aluminium to be used to store reagents such as <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>, concentrated <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, and some organic acids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-43">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>In hot concentrated <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>, aluminium reacts with water with evolution of hydrogen, and in aqueous <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a> or <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">potassium hydroxide</a> at room temperature to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminates" title="Aluminates">aluminates</a>—protective passivation under these conditions is negligible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beal1999_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beal1999-44">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aqua_regia" title="Aqua regia">Aqua regia</a> also dissolves aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-43">[38]</a></sup> Aluminium is corroded by dissolved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlorides" title="Chlorides">chlorides</a>, such as common <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a>, which is why household plumbing is never made from aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beal1999_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Beal1999-44">[39]</a></sup> The oxide layer on aluminium is also destroyed by contact with <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Amalgam_(chemistry)" title="Amalgam (chemistry)">amalgamation</a> or with salts of some electropositive metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227_35-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227-35">[31]</a></sup> As such, the strongest aluminium alloys are less corrosion-resistant due to <a href="/wiki/Galvanic_cell" title="Galvanic cell">galvanic</a> reactions with alloyed <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polmear1995_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Polmear1995-28">[24]</a></sup> and aluminium's corrosion resistance is greatly reduced by aqueous salts, particularly in the presence of dissimilar metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood222_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood222-20">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium reacts with most nonmetals upon heating, forming compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_nitride" title="Aluminium nitride">aluminium nitride</a> (AlN), <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_sulfide" title="Aluminium sulfide">aluminium sulfide</a> (Al<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>), and the aluminium halides (AlX<sub>3</sub>). It also forms a wide range of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intermetallic_compound" title="Intermetallic compound">intermetallic compounds</a> involving metals from every group on the periodic table.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227_35-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227-35">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inorganic_compounds">Inorganic compounds</span></h3>
<p>The vast majority of compounds, including all aluminium-containing minerals and all commercially significant aluminium compounds, feature aluminium in the oxidation state 3+. The <a href="/wiki/Coordination_number" title="Coordination number">coordination number</a> of such compounds varies, but generally Al<sup>3+</sup> is either six- or four-coordinate. Almost all compounds of aluminium(III) are colorless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227_35-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227-35">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlHydrolysis.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="281" data-file-width="278" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/AlHydrolysis.png/220px-AlHydrolysis.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/83/AlHydrolysis.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlHydrolysis.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aluminium hydrolysis as a function of pH. Coordinated water molecules are omitted. (Data from Baes and Mesmer)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[40]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In aqueous solution, Al<sup>3+</sup> exists as the hexaaqua cation [Al(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3+</sup>, which has an approximate <a href="/wiki/Acid_dissociation_constant" title="Acid dissociation constant">K<sub>a</sub></a> of 10<sup>−5</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997242–252_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997242–252-9">[7]</a></sup> Such solutions are acidic as this cation can act as a proton donor and progressively <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolyze</a> until a <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(chemistry)" title="Precipitation (chemistry)">precipitate</a> of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_hydroxide" title="Aluminium hydroxide">aluminium hydroxide</a>, Al(OH)<sub>3</sub>, forms. This is useful for <a href="/wiki/Sedimentation_(water_treatment)" title="Sedimentation (water treatment)">clarification</a> of water, as the precipitate nucleates on <a href="/wiki/Suspension_(chemistry)" title="Suspension (chemistry)">suspended</a> particles in the water, hence removing them. Increasing the pH even further leads to the hydroxide dissolving again as <a href="/wiki/Aluminate" title="Aluminate">aluminate</a>, [Al(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>2</sub>(OH)<sub>4</sub>]<sup>−</sup>, is formed.
</p><p>Aluminium hydroxide forms both salts and aluminates and dissolves in acid and alkali, as well as on fusion with acidic and basic oxides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227_35-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227-35">[31]</a></sup> This behavior of Al(OH)<sub>3</sub> is termed <a href="/wiki/Amphoterism" title="Amphoterism">amphoterism</a> and is characteristic of weakly basic cations that form insoluble hydroxides and whose hydrated species can also donate their protons. One effect of this is that aluminium salts with weak acids are hydrolyzed in water to the aquated hydroxide and the corresponding nonmetal hydride: for example, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_sulfide" title="Aluminium sulfide">aluminium sulfide</a> yields <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>. However, some salts like <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_carbonate" title="Aluminium carbonate">aluminium carbonate</a> exist in aqueous solution but are unstable as such; and only incomplete hydrolysis takes place for salts with strong acids, such as the halides, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_nitrate" title="Aluminium nitrate">nitrate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_sulfate" title="Aluminium sulfate">sulfate</a>. For similar reasons, anhydrous aluminium salts cannot be made by heating their "hydrates": hydrated aluminium chloride is in fact not AlCl<sub>3</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O but [Al(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]Cl<sub>3</sub>, and the Al–O bonds are so strong that heating is not sufficient to break them and form Al–Cl bonds instead:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227_35-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227-35">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2[Al(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]Cl<sub>3</sub> <span class="sfrac nowrap;"><span style="display:none; display:inline-block; vertical-align:middle; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; font-size:70%; padding:0 0.1em;">heat</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">→</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;"> </span></span></span> Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 6 HCl + 9 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>All four <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">trihalides</a> are well known. Unlike the structures of the three heavier trihalides, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_fluoride" title="Aluminium fluoride">aluminium fluoride</a> (AlF<sub>3</sub>) features six-coordinate aluminium, which explains its involatility and insolubility as well as high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_of_formation" title="Heat of formation">heat of formation</a>. Each aluminium atom is surrounded by six fluorine atoms in a distorted <a href="/wiki/Octahedron" title="Octahedron">octahedral</a> arrangement, with each fluorine atom being shared between the corners of two octahedra. Such {AlF<sub>6</sub>} units also exist in complex fluorides such as <a href="/wiki/Cryolite" title="Cryolite">cryolite</a>, Na<sub>3</sub>AlF<sub>6</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[f]</a></sup> AlF<sub>3</sub> melts at 1,290 °C (2,354 °F) and is made by reaction of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">aluminium oxide</a> with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">hydrogen fluoride</a> gas at 700 °C (1,300 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997233–237_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997233–237-46">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>With heavier halides, the coordination numbers are lower. The other trihalides are <a href="/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry)" title="Dimer (chemistry)">dimeric</a> or <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymeric</a> with tetrahedral four-coordinate aluminium centers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[g]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium_trichloride" title="Aluminium trichloride">Aluminium trichloride</a> (AlCl<sub>3</sub>) has a layered polymeric structure below its melting point of 192.4 °C (378 °F) but transforms on melting to Al<sub>2</sub>Cl<sub>6</sub> dimers. At higher temperatures those increasingly dissociate into trigonal planar AlCl<sub>3</sub> monomers similar to the structure of <a href="/wiki/Boron_trichloride" title="Boron trichloride">BCl<sub>3</sub></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium_tribromide" title="Aluminium tribromide">Aluminium tribromide</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium_triiodide" title="Aluminium triiodide">aluminium triiodide</a> form Al<sub>2</sub>X<sub>6</sub> dimers in all three phases and hence do not show such significant changes of properties upon phase change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997233–237_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997233–237-46">[41]</a></sup> These materials are prepared by treating aluminium metal with the halogen. The aluminium trihalides form many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Addition_compound" title="Addition compound">addition compounds</a> or complexes; their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acidic</a> nature makes them useful as <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysts</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Friedel%E2%80%93Crafts_reaction" title="Friedel–Crafts reaction">Friedel–Crafts reactions</a>. Aluminium trichloride has major industrial uses involving this reaction, such as in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Anthraquinone" title="Anthraquinone">anthraquinones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Styrene" title="Styrene">styrene</a>; it is also often used as the precursor for many other aluminium compounds and as a reagent for converting nonmetal fluorides into the corresponding chlorides (a <a href="/wiki/Transhalogenation" title="Transhalogenation">transhalogenation reaction</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997233–237_46-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997233–237-46">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium forms one stable oxide with the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a> Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, commonly called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alumina" title="Alumina">alumina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[42]</a></sup> It can be found in nature in the mineral <a href="/wiki/Corundum" title="Corundum">corundum</a>, α-alumina;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[43]</a></sup> there is also a γ-alumina phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997242–252_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997242–252-9">[7]</a></sup> Its crystalline form, <a href="/wiki/Corundum" title="Corundum">corundum</a>, is very hard (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohs_hardness" title="Mohs hardness">Mohs hardness</a> 9), has a high melting point of 2,045 °C (3,713 °F), has very low volatility, is chemically inert, and a good electrical insulator, it is often used in abrasives (such as toothpaste), as a refractory material, and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceramics" title="Ceramics">ceramics</a>, as well as being the starting material for the electrolytic production of aluminium metal. <a href="/wiki/Sapphire" title="Sapphire">Sapphire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruby" title="Ruby">ruby</a> are impure corundum contaminated with trace amounts of other metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997242–252_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997242–252-9">[7]</a></sup> The two main oxide-hydroxides, AlO(OH), are <a href="/wiki/Boehmite" title="Boehmite">boehmite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diaspore" title="Diaspore">diaspore</a>. There are three main trihydroxides: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bayerite" title="Bayerite">bayerite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gibbsite" title="Gibbsite">gibbsite</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nordstrandite&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nordstrandite (page does not exist)">nordstrandite</a>, which differ in their crystalline structure (<a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(materials_science)" title="Polymorphism (materials science)">polymorphs</a>). Many other intermediate and related structures are also known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997242–252_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997242–252-9">[7]</a></sup> Most are produced from ores by a variety of wet processes using acid and base. Heating the hydroxides leads to formation of corundum. These materials are of central importance to the production of aluminium and are themselves extremely useful. Some mixed oxide phases are also very useful, such as <a href="/wiki/Spinel" title="Spinel">spinel</a> (MgAl<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>), Na-β-alumina (NaAl<sub>11</sub>O<sub>17</sub>), and <a href="/wiki/Tricalcium_aluminate" title="Tricalcium aluminate">tricalcium aluminate</a> (Ca<sub>3</sub>Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>, an important mineral phase in <a href="/wiki/Portland_cement" title="Portland cement">Portland cement</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997242–252_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997242–252-9">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The only stable <a href="/wiki/Chalcogenide" title="Chalcogenide">chalcogenides</a> under normal conditions are <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_sulfide" title="Aluminium sulfide">aluminium sulfide</a> (Al<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>), <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_selenide" title="Aluminium selenide">selenide</a> (Al<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub>), and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Aluminium_telluride&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aluminium telluride (page does not exist)">telluride</a> (Al<sub>2</sub>Te<sub>3</sub>). All three are prepared by direct reaction of their elements at about 1,000 °C (1,800 °F) and quickly hydrolyze completely in water to yield aluminium hydroxide and the respective <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chalcogenide" title="Hydrogen chalcogenide">hydrogen chalcogenide</a>. As aluminium is a small atom relative to these chalcogens, these have four-coordinate tetrahedral aluminium with various polymorphs having structures related to <a href="/wiki/Wurtzite" title="Wurtzite">wurtzite</a>, with two-thirds of the possible metal sites occupied either in an orderly (α) or random (β) fashion; the sulfide also has a γ form related to γ-alumina, and an unusual high-temperature hexagonal form where half the aluminium atoms have tetrahedral four-coordination and the other half have trigonal bipyramidal five-coordination. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997252–257_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997252–257-51">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pnictide" title="Pnictide">pnictides</a> – <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_nitride" title="Aluminium nitride">aluminium nitride</a> (AlN), <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_phosphide" title="Aluminium phosphide">aluminium phosphide</a> (AlP), <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_arsenide" title="Aluminium arsenide">aluminium arsenide</a> (AlAs), and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_antimonide" title="Aluminium antimonide">aluminium antimonide</a> (AlSb) – are known. They are all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/III-V_semiconductor" title="III-V semiconductor">III-V semiconductors</a> isoelectronic to <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>, all of which but AlN have the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc_blende" title="Zinc blende">zinc blende</a> structure. All four can be made by high-temperature (and possibly high-pressure) direct reaction of their component elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997252–257_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997252–257-51">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium alloys well with most other metals (with the exception of most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkali_metals" title="Alkali metals">alkali metals</a> and group 13 metals) and over 150 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intermetallics" title="Intermetallics">intermetallics</a> with other metals are known. Preparation involves heating fixed metals together in certain proportion, followed by gradual cooling and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annealing_(metallurgy)" title="Annealing (metallurgy)">annealing</a>. Bonding in them is predominantly <a href="/wiki/Metallic_bonding" title="Metallic bonding">metallic</a> and the crystal structure primarily depends on efficiency of packing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are few compounds with lower oxidation states. A few <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium(I)" title="Aluminium(I)">aluminium(I)</a> compounds exist: AlF, AlCl, AlBr, and AlI exist in the gaseous phase when the respective trihalide is heated with aluminium, and at cryogenic temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997233–237_46-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997233–237-46">[41]</a></sup> A stable derivative of aluminium monoiodide is the cyclic <a href="/wiki/Adduct" title="Adduct">adduct</a> formed with <a href="/wiki/Triethylamine" title="Triethylamine">triethylamine</a>, Al<sub>4</sub>I<sub>4</sub>(NEt<sub>3</sub>)<sub>4</sub>. Al<sub>2</sub>O and Al<sub>2</sub>S also exist but are very unstable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-al1_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-al1-53">[46]</a></sup> Very simple aluminium(II) compounds are invoked or observed in the reactions of Al metal with oxidants. For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium_monoxide" title="Aluminium monoxide">aluminium monoxide</a>, AlO, has been detected in the gas phase after explosion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[47]</a></sup> and in stellar absorption spectra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[48]</a></sup> More thoroughly investigated are compounds of the formula R<sub>4</sub>Al<sub>2</sub> which contain an Al–Al bond and where R is a large organic <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organoaluminium_compounds_and_related_hydrides">Organoaluminium compounds and related hydrides</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organoaluminium_compound" title="Organoaluminium compound">Organoaluminium compound</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trimethylaluminium-from-xtal-3D-bs-17-25.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="2501" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Trimethylaluminium-from-xtal-3D-bs-17-25.png/220px-Trimethylaluminium-from-xtal-3D-bs-17-25.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Trimethylaluminium-from-xtal-3D-bs-17-25.png/330px-Trimethylaluminium-from-xtal-3D-bs-17-25.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Trimethylaluminium-from-xtal-3D-bs-17-25.png/440px-Trimethylaluminium-from-xtal-3D-bs-17-25.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trimethylaluminium-from-xtal-3D-bs-17-25.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of <a href="/wiki/Trimethylaluminium" title="Trimethylaluminium">trimethylaluminium</a>, a compound that features five-coordinate carbon.</div></div></div>
<p>A variety of compounds of empirical formula AlR<sub>3</sub> and AlR<sub>1.5</sub>Cl<sub>1.5</sub> exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[50]</a></sup> The aluminium trialkyls and triaryls are reactive, volatile, and colorless liquids or low-melting solids. They catch fire spontaneously in air and react with water, thus necessitating precautions when handling them. They often form dimers, unlike their boron analogues, but this tendency diminishes for branched-chain alkyls (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isopropyl" title="Isopropyl">Pr<sup><i>i</i></sup></a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isobutyl" title="Isobutyl">Bu<sup><i>i</i></sup></a>, Me<sub>3</sub>CCH<sub>2</sub>); for example, <a href="/wiki/Triisobutylaluminium" title="Triisobutylaluminium">triisobutylaluminium</a> exists as an equilibrium mixture of the monomer and dimer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997257–67_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997257–67-58">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[52]</a></sup> These dimers, such as <a href="/wiki/Trimethylaluminium" title="Trimethylaluminium">trimethylaluminium</a> (Al<sub>2</sub>Me<sub>6</sub>), usually feature tetrahedral Al centers formed by dimerization with some alkyl group bridging between both aluminium atoms. They are <a href="/wiki/HSAB_theory" title="HSAB theory">hard acids</a> and react readily with ligands, forming adducts. In industry, they are mostly used in alkene insertion reactions, as discovered by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Ziegler" title="Karl Ziegler">Karl Ziegler</a>, most importantly in "growth reactions" that form long-chain unbranched primary alkenes and alcohols, and in the low-pressure polymerization of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethene" title="Ethene">ethene</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propene" title="Propene">propene</a>. There are also some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterocycle" title="Heterocycle">heterocyclic</a> and cluster organoaluminium compounds involving Al–N bonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997257–67_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997257–67-58">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>The industrially most important aluminium hydride is <a href="/wiki/Lithium_aluminium_hydride" title="Lithium aluminium hydride">lithium aluminium hydride</a> (LiAlH<sub>4</sub>), which is used in as a reducing agent in <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>. It can be produced from <a href="/wiki/Lithium_hydride" title="Lithium hydride">lithium hydride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_chloride" title="Aluminium chloride">aluminium trichloride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997227–232_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997227–232-60">[53]</a></sup> The simplest hydride, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_hydride" title="Aluminium hydride">aluminium hydride</a> or alane, is not as important. It is a polymer with the formula (AlH<sub>3</sub>)<sub><i>n</i></sub>, in contrast to the corresponding boron hydride that is a dimer with the formula (BH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997227–232_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997227–232-60">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_occurrence">Natural occurrence</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_bauxite_production" title="List of countries by bauxite production">List of countries by bauxite production</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Space">Space</span></h3>
<p>Aluminium's per-particle abundance in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> is 3.15 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> (parts per million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lodders_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lodders-61">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[h]</a></sup> It is the twelfth most abundant of all elements and third most abundant among the elements that have odd atomic numbers, after hydrogen and nitrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lodders_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lodders-61">[54]</a></sup> The only stable isotope of aluminium, <sup>27</sup>Al, is the eighteenth most abundant nucleus in the Universe. It is created almost entirely after fusion of carbon in massive stars that will later become <a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova" title="Type II supernova">Type II supernovas</a>: this fusion creates <sup>26</sup>Mg, which, upon capturing free protons and neutrons becomes aluminium. Some smaller quantities of <sup>27</sup>Al are created in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_burning" title="Hydrogen burning">hydrogen burning</a> shells of evolved stars, where <sup>26</sup>Mg can capture free protons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clayton_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton-63">[55]</a></sup> Essentially all aluminium now in existence is <sup>27</sup>Al. <sup>26</sup>Al was present in the early Solar System with abundance of 0.005% relative to <sup>27</sup>Al but its half-life of 728,000 years is too short for any original nuclei to survive; <sup>26</sup>Al is therefore <a href="/wiki/Extinct_radionuclide" title="Extinct radionuclide">extinct</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clayton_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton-63">[55]</a></sup> Unlike for <sup>27</sup>Al, hydrogen burning is the primary source of <sup>26</sup>Al, with the nuclide emerging after a nucleus of <sup>25</sup>Mg catches a free proton. However, the <a href="/wiki/Trace_radioisotope" title="Trace radioisotope">trace quantities</a> of <sup>26</sup>Al that do exist are the most common <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma ray</a> emitter in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_gas" title="Interstellar gas">interstellar gas</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clayton_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton-63">[55]</a></sup> if the original <sup>26</sup>Al were still present, <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_astronomy" title="Gamma-ray astronomy">gamma ray maps</a> of the Milky Way would be brighter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clayton_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton-63">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth">Earth</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG/220px-Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG/330px-Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG/440px-Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">Bauxite</a>, a major aluminium ore. The red-brown color is due to the presence of <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">iron oxide</a> minerals.</div></div></div>
<p>Overall, the Earth is about 1.59% aluminium by mass (seventh in abundance by mass).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mit1_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-mit1-64">[56]</a></sup> Aluminium occurs in greater proportion in the Earth's crust than in the Universe at large, because aluminium easily forms the oxide and becomes bound into rocks and stays in the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" title="Earth's crust">Earth's crust</a>, while less reactive metals sink to the core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clayton_63-4"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton-63">[55]</a></sup> In the Earth's crust, aluminium is the most abundant metallic element (8.23% by mass<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cardarelli_2008_p158-163_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cardarelli_2008_p158-163-29">[25]</a></sup>) and the third most abundant of all elements (after oxygen and silicon).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[57]</a></sup> A large number of silicates in the Earth's crust contain aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WadeBanister2016_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-WadeBanister2016-66">[58]</a></sup> In contrast, the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> is only 2.38% aluminium by mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[59]</a></sup> Aluminium also occurs in seawater at a concentration of 2 μg/kg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cardarelli_2008_p158-163_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cardarelli_2008_p158-163-29">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of its strong affinity for oxygen, aluminium is almost never found in the elemental state; instead it is found in oxides or silicates. <a href="/wiki/Feldspar" title="Feldspar">Feldspars</a>, the most common group of minerals in the Earth's crust, are aluminosilicates. Aluminium also occurs in the minerals <a href="/wiki/Beryl" title="Beryl">beryl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cryolite" title="Cryolite">cryolite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Garnet" title="Garnet">garnet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spinel" title="Spinel">spinel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turquoise" title="Turquoise">turquoise</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[60]</a></sup> Impurities in Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, such as <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, yield the <a href="/wiki/Gemstone" title="Gemstone">gemstones</a> <a href="/wiki/Ruby" title="Ruby">ruby</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sapphire" title="Sapphire">sapphire</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KotzTreichel2012_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-KotzTreichel2012-69">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Native_aluminium" title="Native aluminium">Native aluminium</a> metal is extremely rare and can only be found as a minor phase in low oxygen <a href="/wiki/Fugacity" title="Fugacity">fugacity</a> environments, such as the interiors of certain volcanoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[62]</a></sup> Native aluminium has been reported in <a href="/wiki/Cold_seep" title="Cold seep">cold seeps</a> in the northeastern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_slope" title="Continental slope">continental slope</a> of the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>. It is possible that these deposits resulted from <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterial</a> <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduction</a> of tetrahydroxoaluminate Al(OH)<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen_2011_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chen_2011-71">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although aluminium is a common and widespread element, not all aluminium minerals are economically viable sources of the metal. Almost all metallic aluminium is produced from the <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> <a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">bauxite</a> (AlO<sub><i>x</i></sub>(OH)<sub>3–2<i>x</i></sub>). Bauxite occurs as a <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a> product of low iron and silica bedrock in tropical climatic conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[64]</a></sup> In 2017, most bauxite was mined in Australia, China, Guinea, and India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_aluminium" title="History of aluminium">History of aluminium</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="898" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg/170px-Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg/255px-Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg/340px-Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a>, the chemist who first thoroughly described metallic elemental aluminium</div></div></div>
<p>The history of aluminium has been shaped by usage of <a href="/wiki/Alum" title="Alum">alum</a>. The first written record of alum, made by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> historian <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>, dates back to the 5th century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200712_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200712-74">[66]</a></sup> The ancients are known to have used alum as a dyeing <a href="/wiki/Mordant" title="Mordant">mordant</a> and for city defense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200712_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200712-74">[66]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>, alum, an indispensable good in the European fabric industry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClaphamPower1941_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-ClaphamPower1941-75">[67]</a></sup> was a subject of international commerce;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200716_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200716-76">[68]</a></sup> it was imported to Europe from the eastern Mediterranean until the mid-15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>The nature of alum remained unknown. Around 1530, Swiss physician <a href="/wiki/Paracelsus" title="Paracelsus">Paracelsus</a> suggested alum was a salt of an earth of alum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200725_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200725-78">[70]</a></sup> In 1595, German doctor and chemist <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Libavius" title="Andreas Libavius">Andreas Libavius</a> experimentally confirmed this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks1968_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks1968-79">[71]</a></sup> In 1722, German chemist <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hoffmann" title="Friedrich Hoffmann">Friedrich Hoffmann</a> announced his belief that the base of alum was a distinct earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichards18962_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichards18962-80">[72]</a></sup> In 1754, German chemist <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Sigismund_Marggraf" title="Andreas Sigismund Marggraf">Andreas Sigismund Marggraf</a> synthesized alumina by boiling clay in sulfuric acid and subsequently adding <a href="/wiki/Potash" title="Potash">potash</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichards18962_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichards18962-80">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Attempts to produce aluminium metal date back to 1760.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichards18963_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichards18963-81">[73]</a></sup> The first successful attempt, however, was completed in 1824 by Danish physicist and chemist <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted" title="Hans Christian Ørsted">Hans Christian Ørsted</a>. He reacted anhydrous <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_chloride" title="Aluminium chloride">aluminium chloride</a> with potassium <a href="/wiki/Amalgam_(chemistry)" title="Amalgam (chemistry)">amalgam</a>, yielding a lump of metal looking similar to tin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-(København)1827_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-(København)1827-83">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-woehler_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-woehler-84">[76]</a></sup> He presented his results and demonstrated a sample of the new metal in 1825.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200736_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200736-85">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FontaniCosta2014_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FontaniCosta2014-86">[78]</a></sup> In 1827, German chemist <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a> repeated Ørsted's experiments but did not identify any aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venetski_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Venetski-87">[79]</a></sup> (The reason for this inconsistency was only discovered in 1921.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200738_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200738-88">[80]</a></sup> He conducted a similar experiment in the same year by mixing anhydrous aluminium chloride with potassium and produced a powder of aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-woehler_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-woehler-84">[76]</a></sup> In 1845, he was able to produce small pieces of the metal and described some physical properties of this metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200738_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200738-88">[80]</a></sup> For many years thereafter, Wöhler was credited as the discoverer of aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes1936_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes1936-89">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="741" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg/170px-Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg/255px-Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg/340px-Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The statue of <a href="/wiki/Anteros" title="Anteros">Anteros</a> in <a href="/wiki/Piccadilly_Circus" title="Piccadilly Circus">Piccadilly Circus</a>, London, was made in 1893 and is one of the first statues cast in aluminium.</div></div></div>
<p>As Wöhler's method could not yield great quantities of aluminium, the metal remained rare; its cost exceeded that of gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venetski_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Venetski-87">[79]</a></sup> The first industrial production of aluminium was established in 1856 by French chemist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henri_Etienne_Sainte-Claire_Deville" title="Henri Etienne Sainte-Claire Deville">Henri Etienne Sainte-Claire Deville</a> and companions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200739_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200739-90">[82]</a></sup> Deville had discovered that aluminium trichloride could be reduced by sodium, which was more convenient and less expensive than potassium, which Wöhler had used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[83]</a></sup> Even then, aluminium was still not of great purity and produced aluminium differed in properties by sample.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200746_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200746-92">[84]</a></sup> Because of its electricity-conducting capacity, aluminium was used as the cap of the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Monument" title="Washington Monument">Washington Monument</a>, completed in 1885. The tallest building in the world at the time, the non-corroding metal cap was intended to serve as a <a href="/wiki/Lightning_rod" title="Lightning rod">lightning rod</a> peak.
</p><p>The first industrial large-scale production method was independently developed in 1886 by French engineer <a href="/wiki/Paul_H%C3%A9roult" title="Paul Héroult">Paul Héroult</a> and American engineer <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martin_Hall" title="Charles Martin Hall">Charles Martin Hall</a>; it is now known as the <a href="/wiki/Hall%E2%80%93H%C3%A9roult_process" title="Hall–Héroult process">Hall–Héroult process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200755–61_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200755–61-93">[85]</a></sup> The Hall–Héroult process converts alumina into metal. Austrian chemist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Josef_Bayer" title="Carl Josef Bayer">Carl Joseph Bayer</a> discovered a way of purifying bauxite to yield alumina, now known as the <a href="/wiki/Bayer_process" title="Bayer process">Bayer process</a>, in 1889.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200774_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200774-94">[86]</a></sup> Modern production of the aluminium metal is based on the Bayer and Hall–Héroult processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aluminiumleader_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-aluminiumleader-95">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prices of aluminium dropped and aluminium became widely used in jewelry, everyday items, eyeglass frames, optical instruments, tableware, and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_foil" title="Aluminium foil">foil</a> in the 1890s and early 20th century. Aluminium's ability to form hard yet light alloys with other metals provided the metal with many uses at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200764–69_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200764–69-96">[88]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, major governments demanded large shipments of aluminium for light strong airframes;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[89]</a></sup> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, demand by major governments for aviation was even higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seldes2009_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seldes2009-98">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thorsheim2015_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thorsheim2015-99">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks20042_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks20042-100">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the mid-20th century, aluminium had become a part of everyday life and an essential component of housewares.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200769–70_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200769–70-101">[93]</a></sup> In 1954, production of aluminium surpassed that of <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[i]</a></sup> historically second in production only to iron,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[96]</a></sup> making it the most produced <a href="/wiki/Non-ferrous_metal" title="Non-ferrous metal">non-ferrous metal</a>. During the mid-20th century, aluminium emerged as a civil engineering material, with building applications in both basic construction and interior finish work,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov2007165–166_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov2007165–166-106">[97]</a></sup> and increasingly being used in military engineering, for both airplanes and land armor vehicle engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200785_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200785-107">[98]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Earth's first artificial satellite</a>, launched in 1957, consisted of two separate aluminium semi-spheres joined and all subsequent space vehicles have used aluminium to some extent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aluminiumleader_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-aluminiumleader-95">[87]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium_can" title="Aluminium can">aluminium can</a> was invented in 1956 and employed as a storage for drinks in 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov2007135_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov2007135-108">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/w/index.php?title=File:Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg&amp;lang=en"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="630" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg/220px-Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg/330px-Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg/440px-Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World production of aluminium since 1900</div></div></div>
<p>Throughout the 20th century, the production of aluminium rose rapidly: while the world production of aluminium in 1900 was 6,800 metric tons, the annual production first exceeded 100,000 metric tons in 1916; 1,000,000 tons in 1941; 10,000,000 tons in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-102">[94]</a></sup> In the 1970s, the increased demand for aluminium made it an exchange commodity; it entered the <a href="/wiki/London_Metal_Exchange" title="London Metal Exchange">London Metal Exchange</a>, the oldest industrial metal exchange in the world, in 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aluminiumleader_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-aluminiumleader-95">[87]</a></sup> The output continued to grow: the annual production of aluminium exceeded 50,000,000 metric tons in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-102">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_price" title="Real price">real price</a> for aluminium declined from $14,000 per metric ton in 1900 to $2,340 in 1948 (in 1998 United States dollars).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_102-3"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-102">[94]</a></sup> Extraction and processing costs were lowered over technological progress and the scale of the economies. However, the need to exploit lower-grade poorer quality deposits and the use of fast increasing input costs (above all, energy) increased the net cost of aluminium;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi20139_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi20139-109">[100]</a></sup> the real price began to grow in the 1970s with the rise of energy cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi20139–10_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi20139–10-110">[101]</a></sup> Production moved from the industrialized countries to countries where production was cheaper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi201310_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi201310-111">[102]</a></sup> Production costs in the late 20th century changed because of advances in technology, lower energy prices, exchange rates of the United States dollar, and alumina prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi201314–15_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi201314–15-112">[103]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/BRIC" title="BRIC">BRIC</a> countries' combined share in primary production and primary consumption grew substantially in the first decade of the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi201317_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi201317-113">[104]</a></sup> China is accumulating an especially large share of the world's production thanks to an abundance of resources, cheap energy, and governmental stimuli;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi201320_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi201320-114">[105]</a></sup> it also increased its consumption share from 2% in 1972 to 40% in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi201322_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi201322-115">[106]</a></sup> In the United States, Western Europe, and Japan, most aluminium was consumed in transportation, engineering, construction, and packaging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi201323_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi201323-116">[107]</a></sup> In 2021, prices for industrial metals such as aluminium have soared to near-record levels as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2021_global_energy_crisis" title="2021 global energy crisis">energy shortages</a> in China drive up costs for electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The names <i>aluminium</i> and <i>aluminum</i> are derived from the word <i>alumine</i>, an obsolete term for <i>alumina</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[j]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">naturally occurring oxide of aluminium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEDaluminium-origin_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-OEDaluminium-origin-120">[110]</a></sup> <i>Alumine</i> was borrowed from French, which in turn derived it from <i>alumen</i>, the classical Latin name for <a href="/wiki/Alum" title="Alum">alum</a>, the mineral from which it was collected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEDalumine_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-OEDalumine-121">[111]</a></sup> The Latin word <i>alumen</i> stems from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> root <i>*alu-</i> meaning "bitter" or "beer".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2370" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg/170px-The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg/255px-The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg/340px-The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1897 American advertisement featuring the <i>aluminum</i> spelling</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h3>
<p>British chemist <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a>, who performed a number of experiments aimed to isolate the metal, is credited as the person who named the element.
