The first name proposed for the metal to be isolated from alum was <i>alumium</i>, which Davy suggested in an 1808 article on his electrochemical research, published in <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Transactions_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society">Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davy1808_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davy1808-123">[113]</a></sup> It appeared that the name was created from the English word <i>alum</i> and the Latin suffix <i>-ium</i>; but it was customary then to give elements names originating in Latin, so that this name was not adopted universally. This name was criticized by contemporary chemists from France, Germany, and Sweden, who insisted the metal should be named for the oxide, alumina, from which it would be isolated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichards18963–4_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichards18963–4-124">[114]</a></sup> The English name <i>alum</i> does not come directly from Latin, whereas <i>alumine</i>/<i>alumina</i> obviously comes from the Latin word <i>alumen</i> (upon <a href="/wiki/Declension" title="Declension">declension</a>, <i>alumen</i> changes to <i>alumin-</i>).
</p><p>One example was <i>Essai sur la Nomenclature chimique</i> (July 1811), written in French by a Swedish chemist, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jacob Berzelius">Jöns Jacob Berzelius</a>, in which the name <i>aluminium</i> is given to the element that would be synthesized from alum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berzelius_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-berzelius-125">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[k]</a></sup> (Another article in the same journal issue also gives the name <i>aluminium</i> to the metal whose oxide is the basis of <a href="/wiki/Sapphire" title="Sapphire">sapphire</a>.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[117]</a></sup> A January 1811 summary of one of Davy's lectures at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> mentioned the name <i>aluminium</i> as a possibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[118]</a></sup> The next year, Davy published a chemistry textbook in which he used the spelling <i>aluminum</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davy1812_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davy1812-130">[119]</a></sup> Both spellings have coexisted since. Their usage is regional: <i>aluminum</i> dominates in the United States and Canada; <i>aluminium</i>, in the rest of the English-speaking world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEDaluminium-usage_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-OEDaluminium-usage-131">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spelling">Spelling</span></h3>
<p>In 1812, a British scientist, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Young_(scientist)" title="Thomas Young (scientist)">Thomas Young</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[121]</a></sup> wrote an anonymous review of Davy's book, in which he proposed the name <i>aluminium</i> instead of <i>aluminum</i>, which he thought had a "less classical sound".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[122]</a></sup> This name did catch on: although the <i><span class="nowrap">-um</span></i> spelling was occasionally used in Britain, the American scientific language used <i><span class="nowrap">-ium</span></i> from the start.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quinion2005_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quinion2005-134">[123]</a></sup> Most scientists throughout the world used <i><span class="nowrap">-ium</span></i> in the 19th century;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEDaluminium-usage_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-OEDaluminium-usage-131">[120]</a></sup> and it was entrenched in many other European languages, such as <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[l]</a></sup> In 1828, an American lexicographer, <a href="/wiki/Noah_Webster" title="Noah Webster">Noah Webster</a>, entered only the <i>aluminum</i> spelling in his <i><a href="/wiki/Webster%27s_Dictionary#First_edition_1828" title="Webster's Dictionary">American Dictionary of the English Language</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[124]</a></sup> In the 1830s, the <i><span class="nowrap">-um</span></i> spelling gained usage in the United States; by the 1860s, it had become the more common spelling there outside science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quinion2005_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Quinion2005-134">[123]</a></sup> In 1892, Hall used the <i><span class="nowrap">-um</span></i> spelling in his advertising handbill for his new electrolytic method of producing the metal, despite his constant use of the <i><span class="nowrap">-ium</span></i> spelling in all the patents he filed between 1886 and 1903: it is unknown whether this spelling was introduced by mistake or intentionally; but Hall preferred <i>aluminum</i> since its introduction because it resembled <i><a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a></i>, the name of a prestigious metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[125]</a></sup> By 1890, both spellings had been common in the United States, the <i><span class="nowrap">-ium</span></i> spelling being slightly more common; by 1895, the situation had reversed; by 1900, <i>aluminum</i> had become twice as common as <i>aluminium</i>; in the next decade, the <i><span class="nowrap">-um</span></i> spelling dominated American usage. In 1925, the <a href="/wiki/American_Chemical_Society" title="American Chemical Society">American Chemical Society</a> adopted this spelling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEDaluminium-usage_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-OEDaluminium-usage-131">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry</a> (IUPAC) adopted <i>aluminium</i> as the standard international name for the element in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2011_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2011-138">[126]</a></sup> In 1993, they recognized <i>aluminum</i> as an acceptable variant;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2011_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2011-138">[126]</a></sup> the most recent <a href="/wiki/IUPAC_nomenclature_of_inorganic_chemistry_2005" title="IUPAC nomenclature of inorganic chemistry 2005">2005 edition of the IUPAC nomenclature of inorganic chemistry</a> also acknowledges this spelling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[127]</a></sup> IUPAC official publications use the <i><span class="nowrap">-ium</span></i> spelling as primary, and they list both where it is appropriate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[m]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_and_refinement">Production and refinement</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_primary_aluminium_production" title="List of countries by primary aluminium production">List of countries by primary aluminium production</a></div>
<p>The production of aluminium starts with the extraction of <a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">bauxite</a> rock from the ground. The bauxite is processed and transformed using the <a href="/wiki/Bayer_process" title="Bayer process">Bayer process</a> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alumina" title="Alumina">alumina</a>, which is then processed using the <a href="/wiki/Hall%E2%80%93H%C3%A9roult_process" title="Hall–Héroult process">Hall–Héroult process</a>, resulting in the final aluminium metal.
</p><p>Aluminium production is highly energy-consuming, and so the producers tend to locate smelters in places where electric power is both plentiful and inexpensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMP_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-WMP-142">[129]</a></sup> As of 2019, the world's largest <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelters</a> of aluminium are located in China, India, Russia, Canada, and the United Arab Emirates,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-143">[130]</a></sup> while China is by far the top producer of aluminium with a world share of fifty-five percent.
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Resource_Panel" title="International Resource Panel">International Resource Panel</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Metal_Stocks_in_Society_report" title="Metal Stocks in Society report">Metal Stocks in Society report</a>, the global <a href="/wiki/Per_capita" title="Per capita">per capita</a> stock of aluminium in use in society (i.e. in cars, buildings, electronics, etc.) is 80 kg (180 lb). Much of this is in more-developed countries (350–500 kg (770–1,100 lb) per capita) rather than less-developed countries (35 kg (77 lb) per capita).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bayer_process">Bayer process</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bayer_process" title="Bayer process">Bayer process</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_bauxite_production" title="List of countries by bauxite production">List of countries by bauxite production</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">Bauxite</a> is converted to alumina by the Bayer process. Bauxite is blended for uniform composition and then is ground. The resulting <a href="/wiki/Slurry" title="Slurry">slurry</a> is mixed with a hot solution of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a>; the mixture is then treated in a digester vessel at a pressure well above atmospheric, dissolving the aluminium hydroxide in bauxite while converting impurities into relatively insoluble compounds:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannOxide_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannOxide-145">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996643573">.mw-parser-output .block-indent{padding-left:3em;padding-right:0;overflow:hidden}</style><div class="block-indent">Al(OH)<sub>3</sub> + Na<sup>+</sup> + OH<sup>−</sup> → Na<sup>+</sup> + [Al(OH)<sub>4</sub>]<sup>−</sup></div>
<p>After this reaction, the slurry is at a temperature above its atmospheric boiling point. It is cooled by removing steam as pressure is reduced. The bauxite residue is separated from the solution and discarded. The solution, free of solids, is seeded with small crystals of aluminium hydroxide; this causes decomposition of the [Al(OH)<sub>4</sub>]<sup>−</sup> ions to aluminium hydroxide. After about half of aluminium has precipitated, the mixture is sent to classifiers. Small crystals of aluminium hydroxide are collected to serve as seeding agents; coarse particles are converted to alumina by heating; the excess solution is removed by evaporation, (if needed) purified, and recycled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannOxide_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannOxide-145">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Hall.E2.80.93H.C3.A9roult_process"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hall–Héroult_process">Hall–Héroult process</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2856" data-file-width="3027" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg/170px-Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg/255px-Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg/340px-Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Extrusion" title="Extrusion">Extrusion</a> billets of aluminium</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hall%E2%80%93H%C3%A9roult_process" title="Hall–Héroult process">Hall–Héroult process</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_smelting" title="Aluminium smelting">Aluminium smelting</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_aluminium_oxide_production" title="List of countries by aluminium oxide production">List of countries by aluminium oxide production</a></div>
<p>The conversion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alumina" title="Alumina">alumina</a> to aluminium metal is achieved by the <a href="/wiki/Hall%E2%80%93H%C3%A9roult_process" title="Hall–Héroult process">Hall–Héroult process</a>. In this energy-intensive process, a solution of alumina in a molten (950 and 980 °C (1,740 and 1,800 °F)) mixture of <a href="/wiki/Cryolite" title="Cryolite">cryolite</a> (Na<sub>3</sub>AlF<sub>6</sub>) with <a href="/wiki/Calcium_fluoride" title="Calcium fluoride">calcium fluoride</a> is <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolyzed</a> to produce metallic aluminium. The liquid aluminium metal sinks to the bottom of the solution and is tapped off, and usually cast into large blocks called <a href="/wiki/Bar_stock" title="Bar stock">aluminium billets</a> for further processing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-43">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Anodes of the electrolysis cell are made of carbon—the most resistant material against fluoride corrosion—and either bake at the process or are prebaked. The former, also called Söderberg anodes, are less power-efficient and fumes released during baking are costly to collect, which is why they are being replaced by prebaked anodes even though they save the power, energy, and labor to prebake the cathodes. Carbon for anodes should be preferably pure so that neither aluminium nor the electrolyte is contaminated with ash. Despite carbon's resistivity against corrosion, it is still consumed at a rate of 0.4–0.5 kg per each kilogram of produced aluminium. Cathodes are made of <a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">anthracite</a>; high purity for them is not required because impurities <a href="/wiki/Leaching_(chemistry)" title="Leaching (chemistry)">leach</a> only very slowly. The cathode is consumed at a rate of 0.02–0.04 kg per each kilogram of produced aluminium. A cell is usually terminated after 2–6 years following a failure of the cathode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-43">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Hall–Heroult process produces aluminium with a purity of above 99%. Further purification can be done by the <a href="/wiki/Hoopes_process" title="Hoopes process">Hoopes process</a>. This process involves the electrolysis of molten aluminium with a sodium, barium, and aluminium fluoride electrolyte. The resulting aluminium has a purity of 99.99%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_43-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-43">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Electric power represents about 20 to 40% of the cost of producing aluminium, depending on the location of the smelter. Aluminium production consumes roughly 5% of electricity generated in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2011_138-2"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2011-138">[126]</a></sup> Because of this, alternatives to the Hall–Héroult process have been researched, but none has turned out to be economically feasible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_43-5"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-43">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recycling">Recycling</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg/220px-Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg/330px-Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg/440px-Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Common bins for recyclable waste along with a bin for unrecyclable waste. The bin with a yellow top is labeled "aluminum". Rhodes, Greece.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_recycling" title="Aluminium recycling">Aluminium recycling</a></div>
<p>Recovery of the metal through <a href="/wiki/Recycling" title="Recycling">recycling</a> has become an important task of the aluminium industry. Recycling was a low-profile activity until the late 1960s, when the growing use of aluminium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beverage_can" title="Beverage can">beverage cans</a> brought it to public awareness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[134]</a></sup> Recycling involves melting the scrap, a process that requires only 5% of the energy used to produce aluminium from ore, though a significant part (up to 15% of the input material) is lost as <a href="/wiki/Dross" title="Dross">dross</a> (ash-like oxide).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[135]</a></sup> An aluminium stack melter produces significantly less dross, with values reported below 1%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>White dross from primary aluminium production and from secondary recycling operations still contains useful quantities of aluminium that can be <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_dross_recycling" title="Aluminium dross recycling">extracted industrially</a>. The process produces aluminium billets, together with a highly complex waste material. This waste is difficult to manage. It reacts with water, releasing a mixture of gases (including, among others, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>), which spontaneously ignites on contact with air;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[137]</a></sup> contact with damp air results in the release of copious quantities of ammonia gas. Despite these difficulties, the waste is used as a filler in <a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">asphalt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1245" data-file-width="2074" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg/220px-Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg/330px-Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg/440px-Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aluminium-bodied <a href="/wiki/Austin_A40_Sports" title="Austin A40 Sports">Austin A40 Sports</a> (c. 1951)</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal">Metal</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_alloy" title="Aluminium alloy">Aluminium alloy</a></div>
<p>The global production of aluminium in 2016 was 58.8 million metric tons. It exceeded that of any other metal except <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> (1,231 million metric tons).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BGS2018_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-BGS2018-152">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium is almost always alloyed, which markedly improves its mechanical properties, especially when <a href="/wiki/Tempering_(metallurgy)" title="Tempering (metallurgy)">tempered</a>. For example, the common <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_foil" title="Aluminium foil">aluminium foils</a> and beverage cans are alloys of 92% to 99% aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[141]</a></sup> The main <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alloying" title="Alloying">alloying</a> agents are <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Duralumin" title="Duralumin">duralumin</a>) with the levels of other metals in a few percent by weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[142]</a></sup> Aluminium, both wrought and cast, has been alloyed with: <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ross13_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-ross13-156">[143]</a></sup> For example, the <a href="/wiki/Kynal" title="Kynal">Kynal</a> family of alloys was developed by the British chemical manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Chemical_Industries" title="Imperial Chemical Industries">Imperial Chemical Industries</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="532" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg/220px-Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg/330px-Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg/440px-Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium_can" title="Aluminium can">Aluminium can</a></div></div></div>
<p>The major uses for aluminium metal are in:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis199917–24_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis199917–24-157">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Transportation (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">automobiles</a>, aircraft, <a href="/wiki/Truck" title="Truck">trucks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Railway_car" title="Railway car">railway cars</a>, marine vessels, <a href="/wiki/Bicycle" title="Bicycle">bicycles</a>, spacecraft, <i>etc.</i>). Aluminium is used because of its low density;</li>
<li>Packaging (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium_can" title="Aluminium can">cans</a>, foil, frame, <i>etc.</i>). Aluminium is used because it is non-toxic (see <a href="#Toxicity">below</a>), non-<a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">adsorptive</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Splinter" title="Splinter">splinter</a>-proof;</li>
<li>Building and construction (<a href="/wiki/Window" title="Window">windows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Door" title="Door">doors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siding_(construction)" title="Siding (construction)">siding</a>, building wire, sheathing, roofing, <i>etc.</i>). Since steel is cheaper, aluminium is used when lightness, corrosion resistance, or engineering features are important;</li>
<li>Electricity-related uses (conductor alloys, motors, and generators, transformers, capacitors, <i>etc.</i>). Aluminium is used because it is relatively cheap, highly conductive, has adequate mechanical strength and low density, and resists corrosion;</li>
<li>A wide range of <a href="/wiki/Household" title="Household">household</a> items, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cooking_utensil" title="Cooking utensil">cooking utensils</a> to <a href="/wiki/Furniture" title="Furniture">furniture</a>. Low density, good appearance, ease of fabrication, and durability are the key factors of aluminium usage;</li>
<li>Machinery and equipment (processing equipment, pipes, tools). Aluminium is used because of its corrosion resistance, non-<a href="/wiki/Pyrophoricity" title="Pyrophoricity">pyrophoricity</a>, and mechanical strength.</li>
<li>Portable computer cases. Currently rarely used without alloying,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[145]</a></sup> but aluminium can be recycled and clean aluminium has residual market value: for example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Used_Beverage_Can" title="Used Beverage Can">used beverage can</a> (UBC) material was used to encase the electronic components of <a href="/wiki/MacBook_Air" title="MacBook Air">MacBook Air</a> laptop, <a href="/wiki/Pixel_5" title="Pixel 5">Pixel 5</a> smartphone or <a href="/wiki/Montblanc_(company)" title="Montblanc (company)">Summit Lite</a> smartwatch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-patapp_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-patapp-159">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[148]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span></h3>
<p>The great majority (about 90%) of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">aluminium oxide</a> is converted to metallic aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannOxide_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannOxide-145">[132]</a></sup> Being a very hard material (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohs_hardness" title="Mohs hardness">Mohs hardness</a> 9),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lumley2010_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lumley2010-162">[149]</a></sup> alumina is widely used as an abrasive;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mortensen2006_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mortensen2006-163">[150]</a></sup> being extraordinarily chemically inert, it is useful in highly reactive environments such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_pressure_sodium" title="High pressure sodium">high pressure sodium</a> lamps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japan2012_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Japan2012-164">[151]</a></sup> Aluminium oxide is commonly used as a catalyst for industrial processes;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannOxide_145-3"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannOxide-145">[132]</a></sup> e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Claus_process" title="Claus process">Claus process</a> to convert <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> to sulfur in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refineries" title="Refineries">refineries</a> and to <a href="/wiki/Alkylation" title="Alkylation">alkylate</a> <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slesser1988_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Slesser1988-165">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Supp2013_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Supp2013-166">[153]</a></sup> Many industrial <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysts</a> are <a href="/wiki/Catalyst_support" title="Catalyst support">supported</a> by alumina, meaning that the expensive <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyst</a> material is dispersed over a surface of the inert alumina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ErtlKnözinger2008_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-ErtlKnözinger2008-167">[154]</a></sup> Another principal use is as a drying agent or absorbent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannOxide_145-4"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannOxide-145">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArmaregoChai2009_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArmaregoChai2009-168">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3952" data-file-width="2782" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg/170px-Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg/255px-Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg/340px-Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Laser deposition of alumina on a substrate</div></div></div>
<p>Several sulfates of aluminium have industrial and commercial application. <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_sulfate" title="Aluminium sulfate">Aluminium sulfate</a> (in its hydrate form) is produced on the annual scale of several millions of metric tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-169">[156]</a></sup> About two-thirds is consumed in <a href="/wiki/Water_treatment" title="Water treatment">water treatment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-169">[156]</a></sup> The next major application is in the manufacture of paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_169-2"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-169">[156]</a></sup> It is also used as a mordant in dyeing, in pickling seeds, deodorizing of mineral oils, in <a href="/wiki/Tanning_(leather)" title="Tanning (leather)">leather tanning</a>, and in production of other aluminium compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_169-3"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-169">[156]</a></sup> Two kinds of alum, <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_alum" title="Ammonium alum">ammonium alum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_alum" title="Potassium alum">potassium alum</a>, were formerly used as mordants and in leather tanning, but their use has significantly declined following availability of high-purity aluminium sulfate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_169-4"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-169">[156]</a></sup> Anhydrous <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_chloride" title="Aluminium chloride">aluminium chloride</a> is used as a catalyst in chemical and petrochemical industries, the dyeing industry, and in synthesis of various inorganic and organic compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_169-5"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-169">[156]</a></sup> Aluminium hydroxychlorides are used in purifying water, in the paper industry, and as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiperspirants" title="Antiperspirants">antiperspirants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_169-6"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-169">[156]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sodium_aluminate" title="Sodium aluminate">Sodium aluminate</a> is used in treating water and as an accelerator of solidification of cement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_169-7"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-169">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many aluminium compounds have niche applications, for example:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Aluminium_acetate" title="Aluminium acetate">Aluminium acetate</a> in solution is used as an <a href="/wiki/Astringent" title="Astringent">astringent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_Formulary_2008_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_Formulary_2008-170">[157]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aluminium_phosphate" title="Aluminium phosphate">Aluminium phosphate</a> is used in the manufacture of glass, ceramic, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wood_pulp" title="Wood pulp">pulp</a> and paper products, <a href="/wiki/Cosmetics" title="Cosmetics">cosmetics</a>, paints, <a href="/wiki/Varnish" title="Varnish">varnishes</a>, and in dental <a href="/wiki/Cement" title="Cement">cement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[158]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aluminium_hydroxide" title="Aluminium hydroxide">Aluminium hydroxide</a> is used as an <a href="/wiki/Antacid" title="Antacid">antacid</a>, and mordant; it is used also in <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> purification, the manufacture of glass and ceramics, and in the <a href="/wiki/Waterproofing" title="Waterproofing">waterproofing</a> of <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">fabrics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-papich_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-papich-173">[160]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lithium_aluminium_hydride" title="Lithium aluminium hydride">Lithium aluminium hydride</a> is a powerful reducing agent used in <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[162]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Organoaluminium_chemistry" title="Organoaluminium chemistry">Organoaluminiums</a> are used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acids</a> and co-catalysts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[163]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Methylaluminoxane" title="Methylaluminoxane">Methylaluminoxane</a> is a co-catalyst for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ziegler%E2%80%93Natta" title="Ziegler–Natta">Ziegler–Natta</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olefin" title="Olefin">olefin</a> <a href="/wiki/Polymerization" title="Polymerization">polymerization</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Vinyl_polymer" title="Vinyl polymer">vinyl polymers</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyethene" title="Polyethene">polyethene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[164]</a></sup></li>
<li>Aqueous aluminium ions (such as aqueous aluminium sulfate) are used to treat against fish parasites such as <i><a href="/wiki/Gyrodactylus_salaris" title="Gyrodactylus salaris">Gyrodactylus salaris</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AasKlemetsen2011_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-AasKlemetsen2011-178">[165]</a></sup></li>
<li>In many <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a>, certain aluminium salts serve as an immune <a href="/wiki/Immunologic_adjuvant" title="Immunologic adjuvant">adjuvant</a> (immune response booster) to allow the <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> in the vaccine to achieve sufficient potency as an immune stimulant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh2007_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2007-179">[166]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="647" data-file-width="780" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg/290px-Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg/435px-Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg/580px-Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic of aluminium absorption by human skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-health1_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-health1-180">[167]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Despite its widespread occurrence in the Earth's crust, aluminium has no known function in biology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_43-6"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-43">[38]</a></sup> At pH 6–9 (relevant for most natural waters), aluminium precipitates out of water as the hydroxide and is hence not available; most elements behaving this way have no biological role or are toxic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wou_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-wou-181">[168]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_sulfate" title="Aluminium sulfate">Aluminium sulfate</a> has an <a href="/wiki/Median_lethal_dose" title="Median lethal dose">LD<sub>50</sub></a> of 6207 mg/kg (oral, mouse), which corresponds to 435 grams for an 70 kg (150 lb) person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_43-7"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-43">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span></h3>
<p>Aluminium is classified as a non-carcinogen by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Health_and_Human_Services" title="United States Department of Health and Human Services">United States Department of Health and Human Services</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-182">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[n]</a></sup> A review published in 1988 said that there was little evidence that normal exposure to aluminium presents a risk to healthy adult,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gitelman88_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-gitelman88-186">[172]</a></sup> and a 2014 multi-element toxicology review was unable to find deleterious effects of aluminium consumed in amounts not greater than 40 mg/day per kg of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body_weight" title="Body weight">body mass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-182">[169]</a></sup> Most aluminium consumed will leave the body in feces; most of the small part of it that enters the bloodstream, will be excreted via urine;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atsdr_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-atsdr-187">[173]</a></sup> nevertheless some aluminium does pass the blood-brain barrier and is lodged preferentially in the brains of Alzheimer's patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xu92_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-xu92-188">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yumoto09_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-yumoto09-189">[175]</a></sup> Evidence published in 1989 indicates that, for Alzheimer's patients, aluminium may act by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrostatically" title="Electrostatically">electrostatically</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crosslink" title="Crosslink">crosslinking</a> proteins, thus down-regulating genes in the <a href="/wiki/Superior_temporal_gyrus" title="Superior temporal gyrus">superior temporal gyrus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mclachlan15_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-mclachlan15-190">[176]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span></h3>
<p>Aluminium, although rarely, can cause vitamin D-resistant <a href="/wiki/Osteomalacia" title="Osteomalacia">osteomalacia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erythropoietin" title="Erythropoietin">erythropoietin</a>-resistant <a href="/wiki/Microcytic_anemia" title="Microcytic anemia">microcytic anemia</a>, and central nervous system alterations. People with kidney insufficiency are especially at a risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_182-2"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-182">[169]</a></sup> Chronic ingestion of hydrated aluminium silicates (for excess gastric acidity control) may result in aluminium binding to intestinal contents and increased elimination of other metals, such as <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> or <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>; sufficiently high doses (&gt;50 g/day) can cause anemia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_182-3"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-182">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="489" data-file-width="762" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg/290px-Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg/435px-Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg/580px-Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>There are five major aluminium forms absorbed by human body: the free solvated trivalent cation (Al<sup>3+</sup><sub>(aq)</sub>); low-molecular-weight, neutral, soluble complexes (LMW-Al<sup>0</sup><sub>(aq)</sub>); high-molecular-weight, neutral, soluble complexes (HMW-Al<sup>0</sup><sub>(aq)</sub>); low-molecular-weight, charged, soluble complexes (LMW-Al(L)<sub>n</sub><sup>+/−</sup><sub>(aq)</sub>); nano and micro-particulates (Al(L)<sub>n(s)</sub>). They are transported across cell membranes or cell epi-/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothelia" title="Endothelia">endothelia</a> through five major routes: (1) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paracellular" title="Paracellular">paracellular</a>; (2) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcellular" title="Transcellular">transcellular</a>; (3) <a href="/wiki/Active_transport" title="Active transport">active transport</a>; (4) channels; (5) adsorptive or receptor-mediated <a href="/wiki/Endocytosis" title="Endocytosis">endocytosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-health1_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-health1-180">[167]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>During the 1988 <a href="/wiki/Camelford_water_pollution_incident" title="Camelford water pollution incident">Camelford water pollution incident</a> people in <a href="/wiki/Camelford" title="Camelford">Camelford</a> had their drinking water contaminated with <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_sulfate" title="Aluminium sulfate">aluminium sulfate</a> for several weeks. A final report into the incident in 2013 concluded it was unlikely that this had caused long-term health problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium has been suspected of being a possible cause of <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[178]</a></sup> but research into this for over 40 years has found, as of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Aluminium&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, no good evidence of causal effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium increases <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">estrogen</a>-related <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> in human <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer" title="Breast cancer">breast cancer</a> cells cultured in the laboratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[181]</a></sup> In very high doses, aluminium is associated with altered function of the blood–brain barrier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[182]</a></sup> A small percentage of people<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BinghamCohrssen2012_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-BinghamCohrssen2012-197">[183]</a></sup> have contact <a href="/wiki/Allergy" title="Allergy">allergies</a> to aluminium and experience itchy red rashes, headache, muscle pain, joint pain, poor memory, insomnia, depression, asthma, irritable bowel syndrome, or other symptoms upon contact with products containing aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>Exposure to powdered aluminium or aluminium welding fumes can cause <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_fibrosis" title="Pulmonary fibrosis">pulmonary fibrosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[185]</a></sup> Fine aluminium powder can ignite or explode, posing another workplace hazard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exposure_routes">Exposure routes</span></h3>
<p>Food is the main source of aluminium. Drinking water contains more aluminium than solid food;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_182-4"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-182">[169]</a></sup> however, aluminium in food may be absorbed more than aluminium from water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yokel2008_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yokel2008-202">[188]</a></sup> Major sources of human oral exposure to aluminium include food (due to its use in food additives, food and beverage packaging, and cooking utensils), drinking water (due to its use in municipal water treatment), and aluminium-containing medications (particularly antacid/antiulcer and buffered aspirin formulations).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[189]</a></sup> Dietary exposure in Europeans averages to 0.2–1.5 mg/kg/week but can be as high as 2.3 mg/kg/week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_182-5"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-182">[169]</a></sup> Higher exposure levels of aluminium are mostly limited to miners, aluminium production workers, and <a href="/wiki/Kidney_dialysis" title="Kidney dialysis">dialysis</a> patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enviroliteracy_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-enviroliteracy-204">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>Consumption of <a href="/wiki/Antacid" title="Antacid">antacids</a>, antiperspirants, <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a>, and cosmetics provide possible routes of exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChenThyssen2018_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChenThyssen2018-205">[191]</a></sup> Consumption of acidic foods or liquids with aluminium enhances aluminium absorption,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[192]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Maltol" title="Maltol">maltol</a> has been shown to increase the accumulation of aluminium in nerve and bone tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[193]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span></h3>
<p>In case of suspected sudden intake of a large amount of aluminium, the only treatment is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deferoxamine_mesylate" title="Deferoxamine mesylate">deferoxamine mesylate</a> which may be given to help eliminate aluminium from the body by <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">chelation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toxicity_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Toxicity-208">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[195]</a></sup> However, this should be applied with caution as this reduces not only aluminium body levels, but also those of other metals such as copper or iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toxicity_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-Toxicity-208">[194]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_effects">Environmental effects</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2287" data-file-width="3443" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg/220px-Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg/330px-Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg/440px-Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bauxite_tailings" title="Bauxite tailings">Bauxite tailings</a>" storage facility in <a href="/wiki/Stade" title="Stade">Stade</a>, Germany. The aluminium industry generates about 70 million tons of this waste annually.</div></div></div><p>High levels of aluminium occur near mining sites; small amounts of aluminium are released to the environment at the coal-fired power plants or <a href="/wiki/Incineration" title="Incineration">incinerators</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATSDR_Public_Health_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-ATSDR_Public_Health-210">[196]</a></sup> Aluminium in the air is washed out by the rain or normally settles down but small particles of aluminium remain in the air for a long time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATSDR_Public_Health_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-ATSDR_Public_Health-210">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>Acidic <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a> is the main natural factor to mobilize aluminium from natural sources<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_182-6"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-182">[169]</a></sup> and the main reason for the environmental effects of aluminium;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RosselandEldhuset1990_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-RosselandEldhuset1990-211">[197]</a></sup> however, the main factor of presence of aluminium in salt and freshwater are the industrial processes that also release aluminium into air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_182-7"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-182">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>In water, aluminium acts as a toxiс agent on <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gill</a>-breathing animals such as <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> when the water is acidic, in which aluminium may precipitate on gills,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[198]</a></sup> which causes loss of <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">plasma</a>- and <a href="/wiki/Hemolymph" title="Hemolymph">hemolymph</a> ions leading to <a href="/wiki/Osmoregulation" title="Osmoregulation">osmoregulatory</a> failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RosselandEldhuset1990_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-RosselandEldhuset1990-211">[197]</a></sup> Organic complexes of aluminium may be easily absorbed and interfere with metabolism in mammals and birds, even though this rarely happens in practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RosselandEldhuset1990_211-2"><a href="#cite_note-RosselandEldhuset1990-211">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium is primary among the factors that reduce plant growth on acidic soils. Although it is generally harmless to plant growth in pH-neutral soils, in acid soils the concentration of toxic Al<sup>3+</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cation" title="Cation">cations</a> increases and disturbs root growth and function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[202]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">Wheat</a> has <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">developed</a> a tolerance to aluminium, releasing <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> that bind to harmful aluminium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cations" title="Cations">cations</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">Sorghum</a> is believed to have the same tolerance mechanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium production possesses its own challenges to the environment on each step of the production process. The major challenge is the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enviroliteracy_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-enviroliteracy-204">[190]</a></sup> These gases result from electrical consumption of the smelters and the byproducts of processing. The most potent of these gases are <a href="/wiki/Fluorocarbon" title="Fluorocarbon">perfluorocarbons</a> from the smelting process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enviroliteracy_204-2"><a href="#cite_note-enviroliteracy-204">[190]</a></sup> Released <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> is one of the primary precursors of <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enviroliteracy_204-3"><a href="#cite_note-enviroliteracy-204">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>Biodegradation of metallic aluminum is extremely rare; most aluminum-corroding organisms do not directly attack or consume the aluminium, but instead produce corrosive wastes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[205]</a></sup>  The fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Geotrichum_candidum" title="Geotrichum candidum">Geotrichum candidum</a></i> can consume the aluminium in <a href="/wiki/Compact_disc" title="Compact disc">compact discs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[208]</a></sup> The bacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_aeruginosa" title="Pseudomonas aeruginosa">Pseudomonas aeruginosa</a></i> and the fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Cladosporium" title="Cladosporium">Cladosporium</a> resinae</i> are commonly detected in aircraft fuel tanks that use <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a>-based fuels (not <a href="/wiki/Avgas" title="Avgas">avgas</a>), and laboratory cultures can degrade aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[209]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Davy's 1812 written usage of the word <i>aluminum</i> was predated by other authors' usage of <i>aluminium</i>. However, Davy is often mentioned as the person who named the element; he was the first to coin a name for aluminium: he used <i>alumium</i> in 1808. Other authors did not accept that name, choosing <i>aluminium</i> instead. See <a href="#Etymology">below</a> for more details.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">No elements with odd atomic numbers have more than two stable isotopes; even-numbered elements have multiple stable isotopes, with tin (element 50) having the highest number of stable isotopes of all elements, ten. The single exception is <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a> which is even-numbered but has only one stable isotope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEA_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAEA-7">[6]</a></sup> See <a href="/wiki/Even_and_odd_atomic_nuclei" title="Even and odd atomic nuclei">Even and odd atomic nuclei</a> for more details.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Most other metals have greater standard atomic weights: for instance, that of iron is 55.8; copper 63.5; lead 207.2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAAW2013_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIAAW2013-10">[8]</a></sup> which has consequences for the element's properties (see <a href="#Bulk">below</a>)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The two sides of aluminium foil differ in their luster: one is shiny and the other is dull. The difference is due to the small mechanical damage on the surface of dull side arising from the technological process of aluminium foil manufacturing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReynoldsKitchens_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReynoldsKitchens-21">[18]</a></sup> Both sides reflect similar amounts of visible light, but the shiny side reflects a far greater share of visible light <a href="/wiki/Specular_reflection" title="Specular reflection">specularly</a> whereas the dull side almost exclusively <a href="/wiki/Diffuse_reflection" title="Diffuse reflection">diffuses</a> light. Both sides of aluminium foil serve as good <a href="/wiki/Reflectance" title="Reflectance">reflectors</a> (approximately 86%) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible light</a> and an excellent reflector (as much as 97%) of medium and far <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pozzobon_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pozzobon-22">[19]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In fact, aluminium's electropositive behavior, high affinity for oxygen, and highly negative <a href="/wiki/Standard_electrode_potential" title="Standard electrode potential">standard electrode potential</a> are all better aligned with those of <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">scandium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yttrium" title="Yttrium">yttrium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lanthanum" title="Lanthanum">lanthanum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Actinium" title="Actinium">actinium</a>, which like aluminium have three valence electrons outside a noble gas core; this series shows continuous trends whereas those of group 13 is broken by the first added d-subshell in gallium and the resulting <a href="/wiki/D-block_contraction" title="D-block contraction">d-block contraction</a> and the first added f-subshell in thallium and the resulting <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide_contraction" title="Lanthanide contraction">lanthanide contraction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997224–227-35">[31]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">These should not be considered as [AlF<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3−</sup> complex anions as the Al–F bonds are not significantly different in type from the other M–F bonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997233–237_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997233–237-46">[41]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Such differences in coordination between the fluorides and heavier halides are not unusual, occurring in Sn<sup>IV</sup> and Bi<sup>III</sup>, for example; even bigger differences occur between <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">SiO<sub>2</sub></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997233–237_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997233–237-46">[41]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Abundances in the source are listed relative to silicon rather than in per-particle notation. The sum of all elements per 10<sup>6</sup> parts of silicon is 2.6682<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001100000000000000♠"></span>10</span></sup> parts; aluminium comprises 8.410<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000400000000000000♠"></span>4</span></sup> parts.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-104"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-104">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Compare annual statistics of aluminium<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-102">[94]</a></sup> and copper<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_Copper_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS_Copper-103">[95]</a></sup> production by USGS.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-119"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-119">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The spelling <i>alumine</i> comes from French, whereas the spelling <i>alumina</i> comes from Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[109]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-127"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-127">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Davy discovered several other elements, including those he named <i><a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a></i>, after the English words <i><a href="/wiki/Soda_lime" title="Soda lime">soda</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Potash" title="Potash">potash</a></i>. Berzelius referred to them as to <i>natrium</i> and <i>kalium</i>. Berzelius's suggestion was expanded in 1814<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[116]</a></sup> with his proposed system of one or two-letter <a href="/wiki/Chemical_symbol" title="Chemical symbol">chemical symbols</a>, which are used up to the present day; sodium and potassium have the symbols <i>Na</i> and <i>K</i>, respectively, after their Latin names.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-135"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-135">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some European languages, like <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> or <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>, use a different suffix from the Latin <i>-um</i>/<i>-ium</i> to form a name of a metal, some, like <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a> or <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>, have a different base for the name of the element, and some, like <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> or <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, don't use the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a> altogether.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-141"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-141">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For instance, see the November–December 2013 issue of <i>Chemistry International</i>: in a table of (some) elements, the element is listed as "aluminium (aluminum)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[128]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-185"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-185">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">While aluminium per se is not carcinogenic, Söderberg aluminium production is, as is noted by the <a href="/wiki/International_Agency_for_Research_on_Cancer" title="International Agency for Research on Cancer">International Agency for Research on Cancer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldcat_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-worldcat-183">[170]</a></sup> likely due to exposure to polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[171]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1067248974">.mw-parser-output <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><b>Origin:</b> Formed within English, by derivation. <b>Etymons:</b> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r920966791">.mw-parser-output span.smallcaps{font-variant:small-caps}.mw-parser-output span.smallcaps-smaller{font-size:85%}</style><span class="smallcaps">alumine</span><i>n.</i>, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="smallcaps">-ium</span> <i>suffix</i>, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="smallcaps">aluminum</span> <i>n.</i></p></blockquote></span>

<li id="cite_note-OEDalumine-121"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-OEDalumine_121-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.oed.com/view/Entry/5880" rel="nofollow">"alumine, n."</a> <i>Oxford English Dictionary, third edition</i>. Oxford University Press. December 2011. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210611060739/https://www.oed.com/start;jsessionid=2B8662831CD405D28E3F852F18211FD4?authRejection=true&amp;url=%2Fview%2FEntry%2F5880" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 11 June 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><b>Etymology:</b> &lt; French <i>alumine</i> (L. B. Guyton de Morveau 1782, <i>Observ. sur la Physique</i> <b>19</b> 378) &lt; classical Latin <i>alūmin-</i> , <i>alūmen</i> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="smallcaps">alum</span> <i>n.</i><sup>1</sup>, after French <i>-ine</i> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="smallcaps">-ine</span> suffix<sup>4</sup>.</p></blockquote></span>

<li id="cite_note-129"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-129">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1">"Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society of London. For the Year 1810. — Part I". <i>The Critical Review: Or, Annals of Literature</i>. The Third. <b>XXII</b>: 9. January 1811. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hdl_(identifier)" title="Hdl (identifier)">hdl</a>:<a class="external text" href="//hdl.handle.net/2027%2Fchi.36013662?urlappend=%3Bseq=17" rel="nofollow">2027/chi.36013662</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Critical+Review%3A+Or%2C+Annals+of+Literature&amp;rft.atitle=Philosophical+Transactions+of+the+Royal+Society+of+London.+For+the+Year+1810.+%E2%80%94+Part+I&amp;rft.volume=XXII&amp;rft.pages=9&amp;rft.date=1811-01&amp;rft_id=info%3Ahdl%2F2027%2Fchi.36013662%3Furlappend%3D%253Bseq%3D17&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAluminium"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Potassium, acting upon alumine and glucine, produces pyrophoric substances of a dark grey colour, which burnt, throwing off brilliant sparks, and leaving behind alkali and earth, and which, when thrown into water, decomposed it with great violence. The result of this experiment is not wholly decisive as to the existence of what might be called <i>aluminium</i> and <i>glucinium</i></p></blockquote></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-OEDaluminium-usage-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-OEDaluminium-usage_131-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-OEDaluminium-usage_131-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-OEDaluminium-usage_131-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.oed.com/view/Entry/5889" rel="nofollow">"aluminium, n."</a> <i>Oxford English Dictionary, third edition</i>. Oxford University Press. December 2011. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210611060736/https://www.oed.com/start;jsessionid=7486FA56257A57791FB5DF1C726BAE1F?authRejection=true&amp;url=%2Fview%2FEntry%2F5889" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 11 June 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">30 December</span> 2020</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Oxford+English+Dictionary%2C+third+edition&amp;rft.atitle=aluminium%2C+n.&amp;rft.date=2011-12&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.oed.com%2Fview%2FEntry%2F5889&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAluminium"></span>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><p><span class="smallcaps">aluminium</span> <i>n.</i> coexisted with its synonym <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="smallcaps">aluminum</span> <i>n.</i> throughout the 19th cent. From the beginning of the 20th cent., <i>aluminum</i> gradually became the predominant form in North America; it was adopted as the official name of the metal in the United States by the American Chemical Society in 1925. Elsewhere, <i>aluminum</i> was gradually superseded by <i>aluminium</i>, which was accepted as international standard by IUPAC in 1990.</p></blockquote></span>
</li>
